Prominent among those who stood to fill the void was Cnut, "whose leadership of the Scandinavian world gave him a unique influence over the western colonies and whose control of their commercial arteries gave an economic edge to political domination".<sup id="cite_ref-Forte,_et_al.,_&#39;&#39;Viking_Empires&#39;&#39;,_p._227_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forte,_et_al.,_&#39;&#39;Viking_Empires&#39;&#39;,_p._227-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Coinage struck by the king in Dublin, Silkbeard, bearing Cnut\'s quatrefoil type\xe2\x80\x94in issue c. 1017\xe2\x80\x9325\xe2\x80\x94sporadically replacing the legend with one bearing his own name and styling him as ruler either \'of Dublin\' or \'among the Irish\' provides evidence of Cnut\'s influence.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Further evidence is the entry of one <i>Sihtric dux</i> in three of Cnut\'s charters.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In one of his verses, Cnut\'s court poet <a href="/wiki/Sigvatr_%C3%9E%C3%B3r%C3%B0arson" title="Sigvatr \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0arson">Sigvatr \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0arson</a> recounts that famous princes brought their heads to Cnut and bought peace.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This verse mentions Olaf Haraldsson in the past tense, his death at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stiklestad" title="Battle of Stiklestad">Battle of Stiklestad</a> in 1030. It was therefore at some point after this and the consolidation of Norway that Cnut went to Scotland with an army,<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> and the navy in the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_&#39;&#39;Cnut&#39;&#39;._p._102._67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_&#39;&#39;Cnut&#39;&#39;._p._102.-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> in 1031, to receive, without bloodshed, the submission of three Scottish kings: <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_II_of_Scotland#Cnut" title="Malcolm II of Scotland">Maelcolm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macbeth_of_Scotland#Mormaer_and_dux" class="mw-redirect" title="Macbeth of Scotland">Maelbeth</a> and Iehmarc.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> One of these kings, Iehmarc, may be one <a href="/wiki/Echmarcach_mac_Ragnaill" title="Echmarcach mac Ragnaill">Echmarcach mac Ragnaill</a>, an <a href="/wiki/U%C3%AD_%C3%8Dmair" title="U\xc3\xad \xc3\x8dmair">U\xc3\xad \xc3\x8dmair</a> chieftain and the ruler of a sea-kingdom of the Irish Sea,<sup id="cite_ref-Forte,_p._198_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forte,_p._198-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Galloway" title="Galloway">Galloway</a> among his domains.\n</p><p>Furtherly, a <i><a href="/wiki/Lausav%C3%ADsa" title="Lausav\xc3\xadsa">Lausav\xc3\xadsa</a></i> attributable to the <a href="/wiki/Skald" title="Skald">skald</a> <a href="/wiki/%C3%93ttarr_svarti" title="\xc3\x93ttarr svarti">\xc3\x93ttarr svarti</a> greets the ruler of the Danes, Irish, English and Island-dwellers<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94use of <i>Irish</i> here being likely to mean the <a href="/wiki/Norse_Gaels" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse Gaels">Gall Ghaedil</a> kingdoms rather than the <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> kingdoms. It "brings to mind Sweyn Forkbeard\'s putative activities in the Irish Sea and Adam of Bremen\'s story of his stay with a <i>rex Scothorum</i> (? king of the Irish)<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> [&amp;] can also be linked to... Iehmarc, who submitted in 1031 [&amp;] could be relevant to Cnut\'s relations with the Irish".<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_&#39;&#39;Cnut&#39;&#39;._p._102._67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_&#39;&#39;Cnut&#39;&#39;._p._102.-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_the_Church">Relations with the Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cnut_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Relations with the Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg/190px-Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg" width="190" height="368" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg/285px-Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg/380px-Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1302" data-file-height="2521" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canute_and_%C3%86lfgifu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Angels crown Cnut as he and <a href="/wiki/Emma_of_Normandy" title="Emma of Normandy">Emma of Normandy</a><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> (\xc3\x86lfgifu) present a large gold cross to <a href="/wiki/Hyde_Abbey" title="Hyde Abbey">Hyde Abbey</a> in <a href="/wiki/Winchester" title="Winchester">Winchester</a>. From the <i>Liber Vitae</i> in the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Cnut\'s actions as a conqueror and his ruthless treatment of the overthrown dynasty had made him uneasy with the Church. He was already a Christian before he was king\xe2\x80\x94being named <i>Lambert</i> at his baptism<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94although the <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Scandinavia" title="Christianization of Scandinavia">Christianization of Scandinavia</a> was not at all complete. His open relationship with a <a href="/wiki/Concubinage" title="Concubinage">concubine</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfgifu_of_Northampton" title="\xc3\x86lfgifu of Northampton">\xc3\x86lfgifu of Northampton</a>, his <a href="/wiki/History_of_marriage_in_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="History of marriage in Great Britain and Ireland">handfast</a> wife, whom he kept as his northern queen when he wed <a href="/wiki/Emma_of_Normandy" title="Emma of Normandy">Emma of Normandy</a> (confusingly also \xc3\x86lfgifu in Old English), who was kept in the south with an estate in <a href="/wiki/Exeter" title="Exeter">Exeter</a>, was another conflict with Church teaching. In an effort to reconcile himself with his churchmen, Cnut repaired all the English churches and monasteries that were victims of Viking plunder and refilled their coffers. He also built new churches and was an earnest patron of monastic communities. His homeland of Denmark was a Christian nation on the rise, and the desire to enhance the religion was still fresh. As an example, the first stone church recorded to have been built in Scandinavia was in <a href="/wiki/Roskilde" title="Roskilde">Roskilde</a>, c. 1027, and its patron was Cnut\'s sister Estrid.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is difficult to ascertain whether Cnut\'s attitude towards the Church derived from deep religious devotion or was merely a means to reinforce his regime\'s hold on the people. There is evidence of respect for the pagan religion in his praise poetry, which he was happy enough for his <i>skalds</i> to embellish in <a href="/wiki/Norse_mythology" title="Norse mythology">Norse mythology</a>, while other Viking leaders were insistent on the rigid observation of the Christian line, like <a href="/wiki/Olaf_II_of_Norway" title="Olaf II of Norway">St Olaf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Yet he also displays the desire for a respectable Christian nationhood within Europe. In 1018, some sources suggest he was at Canterbury on the return of its Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Lyfing_(Archbishop_of_Canterbury)" title="Lyfing (Archbishop of Canterbury)">Lyfing</a> from Rome, to receive letters of exhortation from the Pope.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> If this chronology is correct, he probably went from Canterbury to the Witan at Oxford, with Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Wulfstan_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Wulfstan II">Wulfstan</a> of York in attendance, to record the event.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_P.87_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_P.87-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>His ecumenical gifts were widespread and often exuberant.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Commonly held land was given, along with exemption from taxes as well as <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Christ Church</a> was probably given rights at the important port of Sandwich as well as tax exemption, with confirmation in the placement of their charters on the altar,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_P.87_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_P.87-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> while it got the relics of <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfheah_of_Canterbury" title="\xc3\x86lfheah of Canterbury">St \xc3\x86lfheah</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> at the displeasure of the people of London. Another see in the king\'s favour was Winchester, second only to the Canterbury see in terms of wealth.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_Minster,_Winchester" title="New Minster, Winchester">New Minster</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Confraternity_book" title="Confraternity book">Liber Vitae</a></i> records Cnut as a benefactor of the monastery,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and the Winchester Cross, with 500 marks of silver and 30 marks of gold, as well as relics of various saints<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> was given to it. <a href="/wiki/Old_Minster,_Winchester" title="Old Minster, Winchester">Old Minster</a> was the recipient of a <a href="/wiki/Shrine" title="Shrine">shrine</a> for the relics of <a href="/wiki/Birinus" title="Birinus">St Birinus</a> and the probable confirmation of its privileges.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> The monastery at Evesham, with its Abbot \xc3\x86lfweard purportedly a relative of the king through \xc3\x86lfgifu the Lady (probably \xc3\x86lfgifu of Northampton, rather than Queen Emma, also known as \xc3\x86lfgifu), got the relics of <a href="/wiki/Wigstan_of_Mercia" class="mw-redirect" title="Wigstan of Mercia">St Wigstan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Such generosity towards his subjects, which his skalds called <i>destroying treasure</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> was popular with the English. Yet it is important to remember that not all Englishmen were in his favour, and the burden of taxation was widely felt.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> His attitude towards London\'s see was clearly not benign. The monasteries at <a href="/wiki/Ely,_Cambridgeshire" title="Ely, Cambridgeshire">Ely</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury" title="Glastonbury">Glastonbury</a> were apparently not on good terms either.\n</p><p>Other gifts were also given to his neighbours. Among these was one to <a href="/wiki/Chartres" title="Chartres">Chartres</a>, of which its bishop wrote: "When we saw the gift that you sent us, we were amazed at your knowledge as well as your faith&#160;... since you, whom we had heard to be a pagan prince, we now know to be not only a Christian, but also a most generous donor to God\'s churches and servants".<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.142-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> He is known to have sent a <a href="/wiki/Psalter" title="Psalter">psalter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sacramentary" title="Sacramentary">sacramentary</a> made in <a href="/wiki/Peterborough" title="Peterborough">Peterborough</a> (famous for its <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illustrations</a>) to <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.146_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.146-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and a book written in gold, among other gifts, to <a href="/wiki/William_V,_Duke_of_Aquitaine" title="William V, Duke of Aquitaine">William the Great</a> of <a href="/wiki/Aquitaine" title="Aquitaine">Aquitaine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.146_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.146-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> This golden book was apparently to support Aquitanian claims of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Martial" title="Saint Martial">St Martial</a>, patron saint of Aquitaine, as an <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Of some consequence, its recipient was an avid <a href="/wiki/Artisan" title="Artisan">artisan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scholarly_method" title="Scholarly method">scholar</a> and devout Christian, and the <a href="/wiki/Abbey_of_Saint_Martial,_Limoges" title="Abbey of Saint Martial, Limoges">Abbey of Saint-Martial</a> was a great <a href="/wiki/Library" title="Library">library</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scriptorium" title="Scriptorium">scriptorium</a>, second only to the one at <a href="/wiki/Cluny" title="Cluny">Cluny</a>. It is likely that Cnut\'s gifts were well beyond anything we can now know.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.146_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Cnut,_p.146-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Cnut\'s journey to Rome in 1027 is another sign of his dedication to the Christian religion. It may be that he went to attend the coronation of Conrad II in order to improve relations between the two powers, yet he had previously made a vow to seek the favour of St Peter, the keeper of the keys to the heavenly kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> While in Rome, Cnut made an agreement with the Pope to reduce the fees paid by the English archbishops to receive their <a href="/wiki/Pallium" title="Pallium">pallium</a>. He also arranged that travellers from his realm not be straightened by unjust tolls and that they should be safeguarded on their way to and from Rome. Some evidence exists for a second journey in 1030.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession">Death and succession</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cnut_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Death and succession">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Cnut died on 12 November 1035. In Denmark he was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Harthacnut" title="Harthacnut">Harthacnut</a>, reigning as Cnut III, although with a war in Scandinavia against <a href="/wiki/Magnus_I_of_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnus I of Norway">Magnus I of Norway</a>, Harthacnut was "forsaken [by the English] because he was too long in Denmark",<sup id="cite_ref-asc_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-asc-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> and his mother <a href="/wiki/Emma_of_Normandy" title="Emma of Normandy">Queen Emma</a>, previously resident at Winchester with some of her son\'s <a href="/wiki/Housecarl" title="Housecarl">housecarls</a>, was made to flee to <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">Bruges</a> in <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a>, under pressure from supporters of Cnut\'s other son, after Svein, by <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfgifu_of_Northampton" title="\xc3\x86lfgifu of Northampton">\xc3\x86lfgifu of Northampton</a>: <a href="/wiki/Harold_Harefoot" title="Harold Harefoot">Harold Harefoot</a> \xe2\x80\x94 <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> in England 1035\xe2\x80\x9337 \xe2\x80\x94 who went on to claim the English throne in 1037, reigning until his death in 1040. Eventual peace in Scandinavia left Harthacnut free to claim the throne himself in 1040 and to regain for his mother her place.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> He brought the crowns of Denmark and England together again until his death in 1042. Denmark fell into a period of disorder with a power struggle between the pretender to the throne <a href="/wiki/Sweyn_II_of_Denmark" title="Sweyn II of Denmark">Sweyn Estridsson</a>, son of Ulf, and the Norwegian king, until the death of Magnus in 1047.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The inheritance of England was briefly to return to its Anglo-Saxon lineage.\n</p><p>The house of Wessex reigned again as <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a> was brought out of exile in Normandy and made a treaty with Harthacnut, his half-brother.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> As in his treaty with Magnus, it was decreed that the throne would go to Edward if Harthacnut died with no legitimate male heir. In 1042, Harthacnut died, and Edward was king. His reign secured Norman influence at Court thereafter, and the ambitions of its dukes finally found fruition in 1066 with <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a>\'s invasion of England and crowning, fifty years after Cnut was crowned in 1017.\n</p><p>If the sons of Cnut had not died within a decade of his death, and if his only known daughter Cunigund, who was to marry Conrad II\'s son Henry III eight months after his death, had not died in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> before she became empress consort,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Cnut\'s reign might well have been the foundation for a complete political union between England and Scandinavia, a North Sea Empire with blood ties to the Holy Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bones_at_Winchester">Bones at Winchester</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cnut_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Bones at Winchester">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Cnut died at <a href="/wiki/Shaftesbury" title="Shaftesbury">Shaftesbury</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dorset" title="Dorset">Dorset</a> and was buried in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Minster,_Winchester" title="Old Minster, Winchester">Old Minster, Winchester</a>. With the events of 1066 the new regime of Normandy was keen to signal its arrival with an ambitious programme of grandiose <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">cathedrals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a> throughout the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Cathedral" title="Winchester Cathedral">Winchester Cathedral</a> was built on the old <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> site and the previous burials, including Cnut\'s, were set in mortuary chests there. During the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> in the 17th century, plundering <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Roundhead</a> soldiers scattered the bones of Cnut on the floor and they were spread amongst the various other chests, notably those of <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William Rufus</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(England)" title="Restoration (England)">restoration of the monarchy</a>, the bones were collected and replaced in their chests, although somewhat out of order.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriages_and_children">Marriages and children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cnut_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Marriages and children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li>1&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/%C3%86lfgifu_of_Northampton" title="\xc3\x86lfgifu of Northampton">\xc3\x86lfgifu of Northampton</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Svein_Knutsson" title="Svein Knutsson">Sweyn Knutsson</a>, King of Norway</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Harold_Harefoot" title="Harold Harefoot">Harold Harefoot</a>, King of England</li></ul></li>\n<li>2&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 <a href="/wiki/Emma_of_Normandy" title="Emma of Normandy">Emma of Normandy</a>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Harthacnut" title="Harthacnut">Harthacnut</a>, King of Denmark and England</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gunhilda_of_Denmark" title="Gunhilda of Denmark">Gunhilda of Denmark</a>, wed <a href="/wiki/Henry_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry III, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry III, Holy Roman Emperor</a>.</li></ul></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_tree">Family solid black; padding: 0.2em;&#32;;"><a href="/wiki/Gyrth_Godwinson" title="Gyrth Godwinson">Gyrth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gunhild_of_Wessex" title="Gunhild of Wessex">Gunhild</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=%C3%86lfgifu_of_Wessex&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="\xc3\x86lfgifu of Wessex (page does not exist)">\xc3\x86lfgifu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leofwine_Godwinson" style="font:1px/1px serif">&#160;</span></div></td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n</div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align: left;"><b>Notes:</b>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-roman;">\n</div>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span id="Cnut.27s_skalds"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cnut\'s_skalds">Cnut\'s <i>skalds</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cnut_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Cnut&#039;s skalds">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Old Norse catalogue of <a href="/wiki/Skald" title="Skald">skalds</a> known as <i><a href="/wiki/Sk%C3%A1ldatal" title="Sk\xc3\xa1ldatal">Sk\xc3\xa1ldatal</a></i> lists eight skalds who were active at Cnut\'s court. Four of them, namely <a href="/wiki/Sigvatr_%C3%9E%C3%B3r%C3%B0arson" title="Sigvatr \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0arson">Sigvatr \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0arson</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%93ttarr_svarti" title="\xc3\x93ttarr svarti">\xc3\x93ttarr svarti</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E%C3%B3rarinn_loftunga" title="\xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3rarinn loftunga">\xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3rarinn loftunga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hallvar%C3%B0r_H%C3%A1reksblesi" title="Hallvar\xc3\xb0r H\xc3\xa1reksblesi">Hallvar\xc3\xb0r h\xc3\xa1reksblesi</a>, composed verses in honour of Cnut which have survived in some form, while no such thing is apparent from the four other skalds <a href="/wiki/Bersi_Sk%C3%A1ldtorfuson" title="Bersi Sk\xc3\xa1ldtorfuson">Bersi Torfuson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arn%C3%B3rr_jarlask%C3%A1ld" title="Arn\xc3\xb3rr jarlask\xc3\xa1ld">Arn\xc3\xb3rr \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0arson jarlask\xc3\xa1ld</a> (known from other works), Steinn Skaptason and \xc3\x93\xc3\xb0arkeptr (unknown). It was these skalds who first referred to his greatness with emphasis on his generosity to the church and military achievements. The principal works for Cnut are the three <i><a href="/wiki/Kn%C3%BAtsdr%C3%A1pa" title="Kn\xc3\xbatsdr\xc3\xa1pa">Kn\xc3\xbatsdr\xc3\xa1pur</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sigvatr_%C3%9E%C3%B3r%C3%B0arson" title="Sigvatr \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0arson">Sigvatr \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0arson</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%93ttarr_svarti" title="\xc3\x93ttarr svarti">\xc3\x93ttarr svarti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hallvar%C3%B0r_H%C3%A1reksblesi" title="Hallvar\xc3\xb0r H\xc3\xa1reksblesi">Hallvar\xc3\xb0r h\xc3\xa1reksblesi</a>, and the <i>H\xc3\xb6fu\xc3\xb0lausn</i> and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=T%C3%B8gdr%C3%A1pa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="T\xc3\xb8gdr\xc3\xa1pa (page does not exist)">T\xc3\xb8gdr\xc3\xa1pa</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E%C3%B3rarinn_loftunga" title="\xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3rarinn loftunga">\xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3rarinn loftunga</a>. Cnut also features in two other contemporary skaldic poems, namely <a href="/wiki/%C3%9E%C3%B3r%C3%B0r_Kolbeinsson" title="\xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0r Kolbeinsson">\xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3r\xc3\xb0r Kolbeinsson</a>\'s <i>Eir\xc3\xadksdr\xc3\xa1pa</i> and the anonymous <i><a href="/wiki/Li%C3%B0smannaflokkr" title="Li\xc3\xb0smannaflokkr">Li\xc3\xb0smannaflokkr</a></i>.\n</p><p>Cnut\'s skalds emphasise the parallelism between Cnut\'s rule of his earthly kingdom and God\'s rule of Heaven.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> This is particularly apparent in their refrains. Thus the refrain of \xc3\x9e\xc3\xb3rarinn\'s <i>H\xc3\xb6fu\xc3\xb0lausn</i> translates to "Cnut protects the land as the guardian of Byzantium [God] [does] Heaven" and the refrain of Hallvar\xc3\xb0r\'s <i>Kn\xc3\xbatsdr\xc3\xa1pa</i> translates to "Cnut protects the land as the Lord of all [does] the splendid hall of the mountains [Heaven]".<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Despite the Christian message, the poets also make use of traditional pagan references and this is particularly true of Hallvar\xc3\xb0r. As an example, one of his half-stanzas translates to "The Freyr of the noise of weapons [warrior] has also cast under him Norway; the battle-server [warrior] diminishes the hunger of the <a href="/wiki/Valcyrie" class="mw-redirect" title="Valcyrie">valcyrie</a>\'s hawks [ravens]."<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The skald here refers to Cnut as "Freyr of battle", a <a href="/wiki/Kenning" title="Kenning">kenning</a> using the name of the pagan god <a href="/wiki/Freyr" title="Freyr">Freyr</a>. References of this sort were avoided by poets composing for the contemporary kings of Norway but Cnut seems to have had a more relaxed attitude towards pagan literary allusions.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cnut_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/North_Sea_Empire" title="North Sea Empire">North Sea Empire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Viking_Age" title="Viking Age">Viking Age</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Raven_banner" title="Raven banner">Raven banner</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cnut_the_Great&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-mother-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-mother_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cnut\'s mother is the subject of historical debate. Some sources identify as her Gunnhilda, others say she is apocryphal or that there is insufficient evidence to name her. According to Medieval chroniclers <a href="/wiki/Thietmar_of_Merseburg" title="Thietmar of Merseburg">Thietmar of Merseburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adam_of_Bremen" title="Adam of Bremen">Adam of Bremen</a>, Cnut was the son of a Polish princess who was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Mieszko_I_of_Poland" title="Mieszko I of Poland">Mieszko I of Poland</a> and sister of <a href="/wiki/Boles%C5%82aw_I_the_Brave" title="Boles\xc5\x82aw I the Brave">Boles\xc5\x82aw I</a>, her name may have been "\xc5\x9awi\xc4\x99tos\xc5\x82awa" (see: <a href="/wiki/Sigrid_the_Haughty#Modern_reconstructions" title="Sigrid the Haughty">Sigrid Storr\xc3\xa5da</a>): this has been linked to Cnut\'s use of Polish troops in England and Cnut\'s sister\'s Anglicized Slavic name <i>Santslaue</i>. Encomiast, <i>Encomium Emmae</i>, ii. 2, p. 18; Thietmar, <i>Chronicon</i>, vii. 39, pp. 446\xe2\x80\x9347; Trow, <i>Cnut</i>, p. 40. The Oxford <a href="/wiki/Dictionary_of_National_Biography" title="Dictionary of National Biography">DNB</a> article on Cnut says her name is unknown. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.oxforddnb.com/view/article/4579">M. K.
